- [00:00] I can be smooth and charming and slick. I can make a very confident impression and
- [00:05] it's hard to leave me at a loss for words.
- [00:07] Sometimes I find myself fantasizing about unlimited success and power, and beauty.
- [00:11] I have repeatedly used deceit to cheat, con, or defraud others for my personal gain. To
- [00:16] be honest, I don't have much concern for the feelings of other people, or their suffering.
- [00:20] Doesn't sound like the Hank you know, does it?
- [00:23] These are all statements from the Self-Assessment measure for Personality Disorders, that lets
- [00:26] patients describe themselves, ranking each statement in terms of how accurate they think it is.
- [00:31] To be honest, you can't rely too much on this kind of self-reporting to assess what we are
- [00:35] talking about today because while some people who are over-confident or obsessed with power
- [00:40] or downright deceitful might tell you that they are, there is a certain subset that won't.
- [00:45] Many of the disorders that we have talked about so far are considered, "ego-dystonic"
- [00:49] meaning that people who have them are aware that they have a problem and tend to be
- [00:53] distressed by their symptoms.
- [00:54] Like a person with Bipolar Disorder or OCD generally knows that they have a psychological
- [00:58] condition and they don't like what it does to them.
- [01:00] But some disorders are trickier then that. They are "ego-syntonic," the person experiencing
- [01:05] them doesn't necessarily think that they have a problem and sometimes, they think the problem
- [01:09] is with everyone else.
- [01:11] Personality disorders fall into this category. These are psychological disorders marked by
- [01:15] inflexible, disruptive, and enduring behavior patterns that impair social and other functioning
- [01:21] -- whether the sufferer recognizes that or not.
- [01:23] Unlike many other conditions that we've talked about, personality disorders are often considered
- [01:27] to be chronic and enduring syndromes that create noticeable problems in life.
- [01:31] And as you can tell from these self assessment statements, they can range from relatively
- [01:34] harmless displays of narcissism, to a true and troubling lack of empathy for other people.
- [01:39] Not only can personality disorders be difficult to diagnose and understand, they can also
- [01:42] be downright scary. Most of the extreme and severe disorders go by names that you probably
- [01:47] recognize: psychopathy and sociopathy. I'm talking, like, serial killers here, mob bosses, Vlad the Impaler.
- [02:04] Cultures have been studying human personality characteristics for thousands of years, but
- [02:09] the concept of personality disorders is a much newer idea.
- [02:12] Much of our modern classifications of these disorders are based on the work of German
- [02:16] psychiatrist, Kurt Schneider, who was one of the earliest researchers into what was
- [02:19] then known as psychopathy and published a treatise on the study in 1923.
- [02:24] Today, the DSM 5 contains ten distinct personality disorder diagnoses, grouped into three clusters.
- [02:30] The first cluster, cluster A, includes what are often labeled simply as "odd" or "eccentric"
- [02:35] personality characteristics. For example, someone with paranoid personality disorder
- [02:39] may feel a pervasive distrust of others and be constantly guarded and suspicious while
- [02:44] a person with a schizoid personality disorder would seem overly aloof and indifferent, showing
- [02:48] no interest in relationships and few emotional responses.
- [02:51] Cluster B encompasses dramatic emotional or impulsive personality characteristics. For
- [02:56] example, a narcissistic personality can display a selfish grandiose sense of self-importance
- [03:00] and entitlement. Meanwhile, a histrionic personality might seem like they're acting a part to get
- [03:05] attention, even putting themselves at risk with dramatic, dangerous, and even suicidal
- [03:10] gestures. The behavior of Cluster B can be truly self-destructive and frightening, and
- [03:14] these disorders are often associated with frequent hospitalization.
- [03:17] Finally, Cluster C encompasses anxious, fearful, or avoidant personality traits. For example,
- [03:22] those with avoidant and dependent personality disorders often avoid meeting new people or
- [03:26] taking risks and show a lack of confidence, an excessive need to be taken care of, and
- [03:30] a tremendous fear of being abandoned. Now, in the past, and, to a great extent, today,
- [03:34] some of these categories have been controversial. Many researchers argue that some of these
- [03:37] conditions overlap with each other so much that it can be impossible to tease them apart.
- [03:42] Narcissistic personality disorder, for example, has many traits that resemble histrionic personality
- [03:47] disorder. And because of this gray area, the most commonly diagnosed personality disorder
- [03:51] is actually personality disorder not otherwise specified or PDNOS. The prevalence of this
- [03:56] diagnosis suggests that while clinicians can identify a personality disorder in a patient,
- [04:01] figuring out the details of the condition can be messy and difficult.
- [04:04] One proposed alternative for diagnosing these disorders is the Dimensional Model, which,
- [04:08] in essence, gets rid of discrete disorders and replaces them with a range of personality
- [04:13] traits or symptoms, rating each person on each dimension. So the Dimensional Model would
- [04:17] assess a patient not with the aim of diagnosing one disorder or another, but instead, simply
- [04:22] finding out that they rank high on say, narcissism and avoidance. It's a work in progress, so
- [04:27] with another generation, the clinical definition of "personality disorder" may evolve pretty radically.
- [04:31] One of the best-studied personality disorders right now is Borderline Personality Disorder,
- [04:36] or BPD. Borderline makes it sounds like patients are like, pretty close to being healthy, but
- [04:40] not quite, but that is not at all the case. BPD sufferers have often learned to use dysfunctional,
- [04:46] unhealthy ways to get their basic psychological needs met, like love and validation, by using
- [04:51] things like outbursts of rage, or on the other end of the spectrum, self-injury behaviors
- [04:56] like cutting or worse. People with BPD were once commonly maligned by clinicians as 'difficult'
- [05:01] or 'attention-seeking', but we now understand BPD as a complicated set of learned behaviors
- [05:06] and emotional responses to traumatic or neglectful environments, particularly in childhood. In
- [05:11] a sense, people with this disorder learn that rage or self-harm helped them cope with traumatic
- [05:16] situations, but as a result, they also end up using them in non-traumatic situations.
- [05:20] Although challenging for patients and clinicians alike, the good news is that some psychotherapies
- [05:25] have helped even the most severely suffering, repeatedly hospitalized BPD patients.
- [05:29] But probably the most famous well-established, and frankly, troubling personality disorder
- [05:33] is Antisocial Personality Disorder. Now, you've heard of this before, but maybe by one of its now
- [05:38] somewhat out of vogue synonyms, "psychopathy" or "sociopathy." People with Antisocial Personality
- [05:43] Disorder, usually men, exhibit a lack of conscience for wrongdoing, even towards friends and family
- [05:49] members. Their destructive behavior surfaces in childhood or adolescence, beginning with
- [05:52] excessive lying, fighting, stealing, violence, or manipulation. As adults, people with this
- [05:57] disorder are thought to generally end up in one of two situations: either they are unable
- [06:01] to keep a job and engage in violent criminal or similarly dysfunctional behavior; or they
- [06:06] become clever, charming con-artists, or ruthless executives who make their way to positions
- [06:11] of power. Tony Soprano would have qualified for a diagnosis, even if he wasn't nearly
- [06:15] as bad as, say, serial killer Ted Bundy or Vlad the Impaler, the infamous 15th century
- [06:20] Romanian prince who personally watched about 100,000 people get impaled or have the skin
- [06:24] of their feet licked off by goats.
- [06:27] Yeah. That happened.
- [06:30] Despite this classic remorselessness, lack of empathy, and sometimes criminal behavior,
- [06:34] criminality is not always a component of antisocial behavior. Certainly many people with criminal
- [06:39] records don't fit that psychopathic profile. Most show remorse, love, and concern for friends
- [06:44] and family. But still, although anti-social personalities make up just about 1% of the
- [06:49] general population, they were estimated in one study to constitute about 16% of the incarcerated population.
- [06:54] So, how might someone end up with such a disturbing disorder? Well, as you might expect, the causes
- [06:59] are probably a tangled combination of biological and psychological threads, both genetic and environmental.
- [07:04] Although no one has found a single genetic predictor of Antisocial Personality Disorder,
- [07:09] twin and adoption studies do show that relatives of those with psychopathic features do have
- [07:13] a higher likelihood of engaging in psychopathic behavior themselves. And early signs are sometimes
- [07:18] detected as young as age three or four, often as an impairment in fear conditioning, in
- [07:23] other words, lower than normal response to things that typically startle or frighten
- [07:27] children like loud and unpleasant noises. Most kids only need to get burned by a hot
- [07:31] dish once to know to stay away, but kids who end up displaying Antisocial Personalities
- [07:34] as adults don't necessarily connect or care about the learned consequences when they're little.
- [07:39] From there, like we've seen in other disorders, genetic and biological influences can intersect
- [07:44] with an abusive or neglectful environment to help wire the personality in a peculiar
- [07:48] and damaged way. While the vast majority of traumatized people don't grow up to be killers
- [07:52] or con-artists, genes do seem to predispose some people to be more sensitive to abuse or trauma.
- [07:58] Meanwhile, studies exploring the neural basis of Antisocial Disorder have revealed that
- [08:02] when shown evocative photographs, like a child being hit or a woman with a knife at her throat,
- [08:07] those with psychopathic personality features showed little change in heart rate and perspiration,
- [08:12] as compared to control groups.
- [08:13] And the classic antisocial lack of impulse control and other symptoms have also been
- [08:17] linked to deficits in certain brain structures. One study compared PET scans from 41 people
- [08:21] convicted of murder to those of non-criminals and found that the convicted killers had greatly
- [08:26] reduced activity in the frontal lobe, an area associated with impulse control and keeping
- [08:30] aggressive behavior in check. In fact, violent repeat offenders had as much as 11% less frontal
- [08:35] lobe tissue than the average brain. Their brains also responded less to facial displays
- [08:39] of stress or anguish, something that's also observed in childhood, so it's possible that
- [08:44] some antisocial personalities lack empathy because they simply don't or can't register
- [08:48] others' feelings. Research has also suggested an overly reactive dopamine reward system,
- [08:53] suggesting that the drive to act on an impulse to gain stimulation or short-term rewards
- [08:57] regardless of the consequences may be more intense than the average person's.
- [09:01] As we mentioned before, because personality disorders are pretty much egosyntonic by definition,
- [09:06] people don't often acknowledge that they have a problem or a need for treatment - and in
- [09:10] the case of Antisocial Personality Disorder, even if they did, there aren't many specific
- [09:14] treatments available, at least not for adults.
- [09:17] But there are some promising interventions for kids and adolescents whose minds and brains
- [09:21] are more plastic and adaptable. In this way, the best way to treat Antisocial Personality
- [09:25] Disorder may be in trying to prevent it. According to American psychiatrist Donald W. Black,
- [09:30] among others, many kids diagnosed with Conduct Disorder, the diagnostic precursor to Antisocial
- [09:35] Disorder, are at high-risk for developing Antisocial Personalities as adults. But by
- [09:40] identifying warning signs early on and by working with these kids and families to correct
- [09:44] their behavior and remove negative influences, some of that impulse fearlessness could be
- [09:49] channeled into healthier directions, like to reward promoting athleticism, or a spirit
- [09:53] of adventure. It's important to remember that Antisocial Personality Disorder is only one
- [09:56] type of personality disorder. This is a diverse family of psychological conditions determined
- [10:01] by many different factors and we're still in the early stages of diagnosing and understanding
- [10:06] the mechanisms behind them.
- [10:08] Today, you learned about personality disorders and the difference between ego-dystonic and
- [10:11] ego-syntonic disorders. We looked at the three clusters of personality disorder, according
- [10:15] to the DSM V, and how personality disorder symptoms often overlap. We also took a look
- [10:20] at Borderline and Antisocial Personality Disorders, including their potential bio-psycho-social roots.
